The Obligation of Knowledge and the Necessity of Speculative Reasoning
The author establishes the obligation of knowledge by consensus and its dependence on speculative reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the obligation to know God is established by consensus and why speculative reasoning (nazar) is a necessary means to that end.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God is not intuitive; it must be acquired through reasoning.

This lesson introduces the foundational premise of Ilm al-Kalam: that knowledge of God is obligatory on every Muslim, and this obligation is so universally recognized that the consensus on it is mutawatir. The author then argues that this knowledge cannot be obtained intuitively but requires speculative reasoning, making reasoning itself obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus
The author begins by stating that the consensus on the obligation of knowledge is mutawatir (massively transmitted). This establishes the certainty of the obligation.
Mutawatir consensus is the highest level of transmitted authority.
2
Examine the analogies
The author compares the obligation of knowledge to obligations like fasting and Hajj, which are not tied to specific premises. This shows that the obligation is absolute and not conditional.
3
Recognize the dependence on nazar
The key point: knowledge is a quality, and obligating its acquisition implies obligating its cause, which is speculative reasoning (nazar). The author gives the analogy of cutting the neck as implied in obligating killing.
This is a causal necessity.
4
Distinguish intuitive from theoretical
The author explicitly states that knowledge of God is not necessary (intuitive) but theoretical, meaning it depends on deliberation.
This refutes the idea that knowledge is self-evident.
5
Conclude the necessity of nazar
Since the absolute duty (knowledge) depends on nazar, and nazar is possible, nazar becomes obligatory by law or reason.
This sets the stage for the debate on the basis of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإجماع على وجوب المعرفة متواتر الاكتفاء (١) إنما كان بالأدلة الإجمالية على أن جواز التبرك للبعض لا ينافي الوجوب في الجملة (٢) واحتياج طرق تحصيل غير الضروري إلى نظر ما ضروري إذ الحكم مختص بالأغلب ومعنى إطلاق الوجوب عدم تقيده بتلك المقدمة، كوجوب الصوم بالنسبة إلى النية والإقامة والحج بالنسبة إلى الإحرام والاستطاعة والمعرفة كيفية لا معنى لإيجابها سوى إيجاب تحصيلها وفيه إيجاب سببها قطعاً، وكحز الرقبة في إيجاب القتل ولـو اكتفينا بالإجماع على وجوبه لكفينا هذه المؤونات . أي لأجل حصولها بقدر الطاقة البشرية لأنه أمر مقدور يتوقف عليه الواجب المطلق الذي ،المعرفة وكل مقدور يتوقف عليه الواجب المطلق فهو واجب شرعاً إذ كان وجوب الواجب) (٣) . المطلق شرعياً كما هو ديناً أو عقلاً، إن كان عقلياً كما هو رأي المعتزلة لئلا يلزم تكليف المحال، أما كون النظر مقدوراً فظاهر، وأما توقف المعرفة عليه فلأنها ليست بضرورية بل نظرية - ولا معنى للنظري إلا ما يتوقف على النظر ويتحصل به وأما وجوب المعرفة فعندنا بالشرع للنصوص الواردة فيه والإجماع المنعقد عليه واستناد جميع الواجبات إليه وعند المعتزلة بالعقل لأنها دافعة لضرر الظنون وهو خوف العقاب في الآخرة حيث أخبر جمع كثير بذلك، وخوف جمع (١) لنقله خلفاً عن سلف يستحيل تواطؤهم على الكذب عادة، بل ينبغي أن يكون هذا مما تواطأت عليه ملل الإيمان جميعاً. وكيف يصح من مؤمن أن لا يقول بوجوب المعرفة التي هي الإيمان بالله تعالى ...؟ ثم لو سلم أن النص ظني والإجماع لنقله الآحاد ظني أيضاً، فالوجوب الشرعي يكفي فيه الظن المفاد لما ذكر.
ما يترتب في الدنيا على اختلاف الفرق في معرفة الصانع من المحاربات وهلاك النفوس وتلف الأموال وكل ما يدفع الضرر المظنون بل المشكوك واجب عقلاً، كما إذا أراد سلوك طريق فأخبر بأن فيه عدو أو سبعاً ورد بمنع ظن الخوف في الأعم الأغلب إذ لا يلزم الشعور بالاختلاف وبما يترتب عليه من الضرر ولا بالصانع وبما رتب في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب والإخبار بذلك إنما يصل إلى البعض وعلى تقدير الوصول لا رجحان لجانب الصدق لأن التقدير عدم معرفة الصانع وبعثة الأنبياء ودلالة المعجزات، ولو سلم ظن الخوف فلا نسلم أن تحصيل المعرفة يدفعه لأن احتمال الخطأ قائم فخوف العقاب أو الاختلاف بحاله والعناء لزيادة .
And the consensus on the obligation of knowledge is mutawatir. Acquiring it is only through general proofs. That permission of blessing for some does not negate obligation in general. And the dependence of methods of acquiring non-intuitive [knowledge] on inevitable speculative reasoning, for the judgment is specific to the majority. The meaning of absolute obligation is that it is not restricted by that premise, like the obligation of fasting in relation to intention and residence, and Hajj in relation to ihram and ability. Knowledge is a quality whose obligation has no meaning other than obligating its acquisition, and that includes obligating its cause necessarily, like cutting the neck in obligating killing. And if we sufficed with consensus on its obligation, we would be spared these troubles. That is, for achieving it to the extent of human ability, because it is a possible matter on which the absolute duty depends. And every possible matter on which the absolute duty depends is obligatory by law, if the obligation of the absolute duty is legal, as it is [according to us] religiously, or [if it is] rational, as is the view of the Mu'tazila, to avoid the impossible. That speculative reasoning is possible is apparent. And that knowledge depends on it because it is not necessary but theoretical – and theoretical only means what depends on speculation and is obtained through it. And the obligation of knowledge is according to us by revelation, because of the texts concerning it and the consensus established upon it and all duties being based on it; whereas according to the Mu'tazila it is by reason, because it repels harm of conjectures, which is fear of punishment in the Hereafter, when a large group has reported that. And fear of what results in this world from the differences of sects concerning knowledge of the Creator – wars, destruction of souls, waste of wealth – and everything that repels conjectured or even doubted harm is rationally obligatory. Just as if one intends to travel a road and is told there is an enemy or a beast, it is replied by denying the fear of harm in most cases, because there is no necessary awareness of the disagreement and what follows from it of harm, nor of the Creator and what is arranged in the Hereafter of reward and punishment. Reporting that only reaches some. And assuming it reaches, there is no preponderance of truthfulness because the assumption is lack of knowledge of the Creator, sending of prophets, and evidence of miracles. And if fear is conceded, we do not concede that acquiring knowledge repels it because the possibility of error remains, so fear of punishment or disagreement remains, and trouble increases.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the central argument for the obligation of knowledge (ma'rifa) of God and the necessity of speculative reasoning (nazar). The author begins by stating that consensus (ijmā') on the obligation of knowledge is mutawatir (massively transmitted). Acquiring it is through general proofs. The permission of blessing for some does not negate the general obligation. The dependence of methods for acquiring non-intuitive knowledge on speculative reasoning is necessary, because the judgment is specific to the majority. The meaning of absolute obligation is that it is not tied to specific premises, like fasting does not depend on intention and residence. Knowledge is a quality; its obligation means only obligating its acquisition, which necessarily obligates its cause. This is analogous to cutting the neck being implied in obligating killing. The author then explains that if we sufficed with consensus on obligation, these discussions would be unnecessary. Knowledge is achievable to human capacity and is a possible matter on which the absolute duty depends; therefore it becomes obligatory by law (shar'). If the obligation is legal (shar'ī) as we hold, or rational ('aqlī) as the Mu'tazila hold. Speculative reasoning is clearly possible, and knowledge depends on it because it is not necessary (ḍarūrī) but theoretical (naẓarī). Theoretical only means what depends on speculation. The author then contrasts the Sunni and Mu'tazilī positions: for Sunnis, obligation of knowledge is from revelation (naṣṣ) and consensus; for Mu'tazila, from reason because it repels probable harm, i.e., fear of punishment in the Hereafter reported by many. The author then addresses the Mu'tazilī proof by reason, stating that the fear mentioned is not certain; it depends on awareness of disagreements and consequences, which is not necessarily present. Reporting reaches only some, and even if it reaches, there is no reason to prefer truthfulness because the assumed state is ignorance of the Creator, prophets, and miracles. Even if fear is conceded, acquiring knowledge does not necessarily remove it because error is possible, so fear remains and trouble increases.

Key Terms

النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of God is self-evident and requires no reasoning.
Correction: The passage states that knowledge of God is not necessary (ḍarūrī) but theoretical, meaning it depends on speculative reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is speculative reasoning (nazar) obligatory?
A) Because it is intuitive.
B) Because knowledge depends on it and knowledge is obligatory.
C) Because the Mu'tazila said so.
D) Because of the consensus alone.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God is not self-evident; it requires speculative reasoning, which becomes obligatory as the means to fulfill the obligation of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says obligating knowledge obligates its cause. In your daily life, do you treat the means of seeking knowledge (like study, contemplation, debate) as optional extras or as binding duties? What would change if you treated them as obligations?
?
If your faith in God were truly based on reasoning rather than comfort, would you be more open to challenging questions? What is one objection to your beliefs you have been avoiding, and why?

Rational Judgments

Revelation versus Reason: The Basis of Obligation and the Rebuttal of Rational Argument
The author presents the Sunni and Mu'tazila positions on the basis of obligation and refutes the Mu'tazila rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting positions of Sunnis and Mu'tazila on the basis of the obligation to know God, and the author's rebuttal of the rational argument.

Core Idea
The obligation to know God is from revelation, not reason; the rational argument fails due to uncertainty.

After establishing that speculative reasoning is necessary, the author now addresses a more fundamental question: what makes knowledge of God obligatory? The Sunni position is that it comes from revelation (al-shar') and consensus, while the Mu'tazila argue it is rationally obligatory to avoid harm. The author then systematically refutes the rational argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The author contrasts the Sunni view (obligation from revelation) with the Mu'tazila view (obligation from reason). Note the sources of authority: texts and consensus vs. rational harm-repulsion.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila proof
The Mu'tazila argue that fearing punishment in the Hereafter, reported by many, makes knowledge obligatory. Also, worldly harm from disagreement on God's existence also rationally obligates.
They use the principle that avoiding probable harm is rationally required.
3
Analyze the first rebuttal
The author counters that most people are not aware of the disagreements or the reports. Even if reports reach, there is no reason to trust them without prior knowledge of God and prophets.
This undermines the certainty of the fear.
4
Consider the second rebuttal
Even if fear is conceded, acquiring knowledge does not necessarily remove it because error is possible. So the harm remains and effort increases.
This shows the rational argument does not lead to the desired outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإجماع على وجوب المعرفة متواتر الاكتفاء (١) إنما كان بالأدلة الإجمالية على أن جواز التبرك للبعض لا ينافي الوجوب في الجملة (٢) واحتياج طرق تحصيل غير الضروري إلى نظر ما ضروري إذ الحكم مختص بالأغلب ومعنى إطلاق الوجوب عدم تقيده بتلك المقدمة، كوجوب الصوم بالنسبة إلى النية والإقامة والحج بالنسبة إلى الإحرام والاستطاعة والمعرفة كيفية لا معنى لإيجابها سوى إيجاب تحصيلها وفيه إيجاب سببها قطعاً، وكحز الرقبة في إيجاب القتل ولـو اكتفينا بالإجماع على وجوبه لكفينا هذه المؤونات . أي لأجل حصولها بقدر الطاقة البشرية لأنه أمر مقدور يتوقف عليه الواجب المطلق الذي ،المعرفة وكل مقدور يتوقف عليه الواجب المطلق فهو واجب شرعاً إذ كان وجوب الواجب) (٣) . المطلق شرعياً كما هو ديناً أو عقلاً، إن كان عقلياً كما هو رأي المعتزلة لئلا يلزم تكليف المحال، أما كون النظر مقدوراً فظاهر، وأما توقف المعرفة عليه فلأنها ليست بضرورية بل نظرية - ولا معنى للنظري إلا ما يتوقف على النظر ويتحصل به وأما وجوب المعرفة فعندنا بالشرع للنصوص الواردة فيه والإجماع المنعقد عليه واستناد جميع الواجبات إليه وعند المعتزلة بالعقل لأنها دافعة لضرر الظنون وهو خوف العقاب في الآخرة حيث أخبر جمع كثير بذلك، وخوف جمع (١) لنقله خلفاً عن سلف يستحيل تواطؤهم على الكذب عادة، بل ينبغي أن يكون هذا مما تواطأت عليه ملل الإيمان جميعاً. وكيف يصح من مؤمن أن لا يقول بوجوب المعرفة التي هي الإيمان بالله تعالى ...؟ ثم لو سلم أن النص ظني والإجماع لنقله الآحاد ظني أيضاً، فالوجوب الشرعي يكفي فيه الظن المفاد لما ذكر.
ما يترتب في الدنيا على اختلاف الفرق في معرفة الصانع من المحاربات وهلاك النفوس وتلف الأموال وكل ما يدفع الضرر المظنون بل المشكوك واجب عقلاً، كما إذا أراد سلوك طريق فأخبر بأن فيه عدو أو سبعاً ورد بمنع ظن الخوف في الأعم الأغلب إذ لا يلزم الشعور بالاختلاف وبما يترتب عليه من الضرر ولا بالصانع وبما رتب في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب والإخبار بذلك إنما يصل إلى البعض وعلى تقدير الوصول لا رجحان لجانب الصدق لأن التقدير عدم معرفة الصانع وبعثة الأنبياء ودلالة المعجزات، ولو سلم ظن الخوف فلا نسلم أن تحصيل المعرفة يدفعه لأن احتمال الخطأ قائم فخوف العقاب أو الاختلاف بحاله والعناء لزيادة .
And the consensus on the obligation of knowledge is mutawatir. Acquiring it is only through general proofs. That permission of blessing for some does not negate obligation in general. And the dependence of methods of acquiring non-intuitive [knowledge] on inevitable speculative reasoning, for the judgment is specific to the majority. The meaning of absolute obligation is that it is not restricted by that premise, like the obligation of fasting in relation to intention and residence, and Hajj in relation to ihram and ability. Knowledge is a quality whose obligation has no meaning other than obligating its acquisition, and that includes obligating its cause necessarily, like cutting the neck in obligating killing. And if we sufficed with consensus on its obligation, we would be spared these troubles. That is, for achieving it to the extent of human ability, because it is a possible matter on which the absolute duty depends. And every possible matter on which the absolute duty depends is obligatory by law, if the obligation of the absolute duty is legal, as it is [according to us] religiously, or [if it is] rational, as is the view of the Mu'tazila, to avoid the impossible. That speculative reasoning is possible is apparent. And that knowledge depends on it because it is not necessary but theoretical – and theoretical only means what depends on speculation and is obtained through it. And the obligation of knowledge is according to us by revelation, because of the texts concerning it and the consensus established upon it and all duties being based on it; whereas according to the Mu'tazila it is by reason, because it repels harm of conjectures, which is fear of punishment in the Hereafter, when a large group has reported that. And fear of what results in this world from the differences of sects concerning knowledge of the Creator – wars, destruction of souls, waste of wealth – and everything that repels conjectured or even doubted harm is rationally obligatory. Just as if one intends to travel a road and is told there is an enemy or a beast, it is replied by denying the fear of harm in most cases, because there is no necessary awareness of the disagreement and what follows from it of harm, nor of the Creator and what is arranged in the Hereafter of reward and punishment. Reporting that only reaches some. And assuming it reaches, there is no preponderance of truthfulness because the assumption is lack of knowledge of the Creator, sending of prophets, and evidence of miracles. And if fear is conceded, we do not concede that acquiring knowledge repels it because the possibility of error remains, so fear of punishment or disagreement remains, and trouble increases.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents the central argument for the obligation of knowledge (ma'rifa) of God and the necessity of speculative reasoning (nazar). The author begins by stating that consensus (ijmā') on the obligation of knowledge is mutawatir (massively transmitted). Acquiring it is through general proofs. The permission of blessing for some does not negate the general obligation. The dependence of methods for acquiring non-intuitive knowledge on speculative reasoning is necessary, because the judgment is specific to the majority. The meaning of absolute obligation is that it is not tied to specific premises, like fasting does not depend on intention and residence. Knowledge is a quality; its obligation means only obligating its acquisition, which necessarily obligates its cause. This is analogous to cutting the neck being implied in obligating killing. The author then explains that if we sufficed with consensus on obligation, these discussions would be unnecessary. Knowledge is achievable to human capacity and is a possible matter on which the absolute duty depends; therefore it becomes obligatory by law (shar'). If the obligation is legal (shar'ī) as we hold, or rational ('aqlī) as the Mu'tazila hold. Speculative reasoning is clearly possible, and knowledge depends on it because it is not necessary (ḍarūrī) but theoretical (naẓarī). Theoretical only means what depends on speculation. The author then contrasts the Sunni and Mu'tazilī positions: for Sunnis, obligation of knowledge is from revelation (naṣṣ) and consensus; for Mu'tazila, from reason because it repels probable harm, i.e., fear of punishment in the Hereafter reported by many. The author then addresses the Mu'tazilī proof by reason, stating that the fear mentioned is not certain; it depends on awareness of disagreements and consequences, which is not necessarily present. Reporting reaches only some, and even if it reaches, there is no reason to prefer truthfulness because the assumed state is ignorance of the Creator, prophets, and miracles. Even if fear is conceded, acquiring knowledge does not necessarily remove it because error is possible, so fear remains and trouble increases.

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position grounds the obligation of knowledge in revelation and consensus, and the Mu'tazila rational argument is refuted by showing the uncertainty of the feared harm and the inefficacy of knowledge to remove it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you commit to grounding your obligation firmly in the revealed texts, not your own reasoning?
?

How might prioritizing revelation over reason in your own heart prevent such division? What step will you take today to unite behind the revelation-based approach advocated here?

Responding to the Objection of Knowledge's Benefit and Introducing the Five Objections
Refutation of an objection and categorization of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that acquiring knowledge always makes one better and why he categorizes objections into general and specific types for the proof of nazar.

Core Idea
Knowledge can lead to salvation or ruin; the manner of seeking it matters.

Before presenting the specific objections against the proof for nazar, the author addresses a preliminary objection: that acquiring knowledge makes one better, implying its obligation. He gives a nuanced answer, reminding us that knowledge must be sought correctly. This passage then sets the stage by distinguishing between objections that are not unique to this proof and those that are, and announces five specific ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the initial objection starts with 'فإن قيل'.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Find the objection that obtaining knowledge makes one better and the author's response.
The author concedes the premise but adds a condition.
3
Understand the categorization of objections
Notice that the author mentions some objections are not specific to this proof and do not need resolution, while others are specific and need refutation. He then says there are five such specific objections.
The five are not listed in this passage but are introduced here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا شك أن من حصل المعرفة أحسن حالاً ممن لم يحصل لانصافه بالكمال وتحصيل الأحسن واجب في نظر العقل . قلنا : نعم إذا حصلت المعرفة على وجهها ولا قطع بذلك بل ربما يحصل (۱) ويقع في أودية الضلال فيهلك. ولهذا قيل البلاهة (٢) أدنى إلى الخلاص من فطانة (۳) بتراء ، هذا بعد تسليم وجوب الأحسن وتقرير السؤال على ما ذكرنا تتميم للدليل المذكور لبيان وجوب المعرفة وعلى ما في المواقف وهو أن الناظر أحسن حالاً ابتداء دليل على وجوب النظر عقلاً، وأورد على هذا الاستدلال إشكالات بعضها غير مختص به ولا مفتقر إلى حله، لكونه منعاً على مقدمات مثبتة(٤) مقررة، مثل إفادة النظر العلم مطلقاً وفي الإلهيات وبلا معلم وإمكان تحقق الإجماع ونقله، وكونه حجة وبعضها مختص به مفتقر إلى دفعه . وهي خمسة :
For it is said: There is no doubt that whoever obtains knowledge is in a better state than one who does not, because of his perfection, and obtaining what is better is obligatory in the view of reason. We say: Yes, if knowledge is obtained in its proper manner, but there is no certainty of that; rather, perhaps he obtains it and falls into the valleys of misguidance and perishes. Hence it is said: Stupidity is nearer to salvation than a truncated intelligence. This after conceding the obligation of what is better and establishing the question as we have mentioned is a completion of the aforementioned proof to clarify the obligation of knowledge. And according to what is in al-Mawaqif, that the speculative reasoner is in a better state initially is a proof for the rational obligation of nazar. And objections were raised against this inference, some of which are not specific to it and do not need resolution, because they are denials against established premises, such as that nazar yields knowledge absolutely, in metaphysics and without a teacher, and the possibility of consensus and its transmission, and its being a proof. And some are specific to it and require refutation. They are five.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer, then introduces a set of five specific objections. The objection states that no doubt the one who obtains knowledge is better than the one who does not, because of perfection, and obtaining the better is rationally obligatory. The author answers: Yes, if knowledge is obtained correctly, but there is no certainty of that; rather, one may obtain it and fall into misguidance and perish. Hence the saying: stupidity is nearer to salvation than a truncated intelligence. This is after conceding the obligation of what is better and establishing the question as mentioned, as a completion of the proof for the obligation of knowledge. According to al-Mawaqif, the fact that the speculative reasoner is in a better state initially is a proof that nazar is rationally obligatory. Objections were raised against this inference. Some objections are not specific to this proof and do not need resolution because they are denials against established premises (e.g., that nazar yields knowledge absolutely, in metaphysical matters and without a teacher, the possibility of consensus and its transmission, and its being a proof). Other objections are specific to this proof and require refutation. The author then mentions that these specific objections are five in number (though they are not enumerated in this passage).

الأحسن
الإشكالات

Correction: The author clarifies that knowledge must be sought correctly; otherwise it may lead to deeper misguidance and perdition.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is not automatically beneficial; it must be sought correctly, and objections against the proof for nazar are of two types: general and specific, with five specific ones to be addressed.

The Obligation to Know God from Harm Avoidance
Sanusi argues obligation of knowing God from harm avoidance, with objection and reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the obligation to know God based on avoiding harm, including how Sanusi responds to a key objection.

Core Idea
Avoiding harm obliges knowledge of God, but only if pursued correctly.

Sanusi presents a rational argument that avoiding worldly harm makes it obligatory to seek knowledge of the Creator. He anticipates an objection about uncertainty and shows that proper knowledge is what truly benefits. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and his response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a general principle, an analogy, an objection, and a reply.
2
Identify the principle of harm avoidance
Find where Sanusi states that repelling suspected harm is rationally obligatory. Notice the analogy with a traveler warned of danger.
3
Trace the objection
Look for the phrase 'فإن قيل' which introduces an objection. The objector argues that knowledge always improves one's state.
4
Understand the reply
Read Sanusi's response starting with 'قلنا'. He qualifies that only correct knowledge is beneficial; without certainty it can lead to misguidance.
The story of a defective intellect (fiṭāna batrāʾ) emphasizes the danger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يترتب في الدنيا على اختلاف الفرق في معرفة الصانع من المحاربات وهلاك النفوس وتلف الأموال وكل ما يدفع الضرر المظنون بل المشكوك واجب عقلاً، كما إذا أراد سلوك طريق فأخبر بأن فيه عدو أو سبعاً ورد بمنع ظن الخوف في الأعم الأغلب إذ لا يلزم الشعور بالاختلاف وبما يترتب عليه من الضرر ولا بالصانع وبما رتب في الآخرة من الثواب والعقاب والإخبار بذلك إنما يصل إلى البعض وعلى تقدير الوصول لا رجحان لجانب الصدق لأن التقدير عدم معرفة الصانع وبعثة الأنبياء ودلالة المعجزات، ولو سلم ظن الخوف فلا نسلم أن تحصيل المعرفة يدفعه لأن احتمال الخطأ قائم فخوف العقاب أو الاختلاف بحاله والعناء لزيادة . فإن قيل : لا شك أن من حصل المعرفة أحسن حالاً ممن لم يحصل لانصافه بالكمال وتحصيل الأحسن واجب في نظر العقل . قلنا : نعم إذا حصلت المعرفة على وجهها ولا قطع بذلك بل ربما يحصل ويقع في أودية الضلال فيهلك. ولهذا قيل البلاهة أدنى إلى الخلاص من فطانة بتراء
What occurs in this world due to the disagreement of sects concerning knowledge of the Maker (ma'rifat al-Sani')—such as wars, destruction of souls, and waste of wealth—and everything that repels suspected harm, even doubtful harm, is rationally obligatory (wājib ʿaqlan). Just as if someone wants to travel a road and is told that there is an enemy or a beast on it, and the response is that the fear of harm is not preponderant in the most common cases, since awareness of disagreement and its consequent harm is not necessary, nor of the Maker, nor of what is arranged in the Hereafter of reward and punishment; moreover, information about that only reaches some, and assuming it reaches, there is no preponderance for the side of truth because the assumption is that there is no knowledge of the Maker, the sending of prophets, and the evidence of miracles. And even if we grant the fear of harm, we do not grant that acquiring knowledge repels it, because the possibility of error remains, so the fear of punishment or disagreement persists, and the trouble is increased. If it is said: There is no doubt that whoever acquires knowledge is in a better state than whoever does not, because he is adorned with perfection, and acquiring what is better is obligatory in the view of reason. We say: Yes, if knowledge is acquired in its proper manner, but there is no certainty of that; rather, perhaps one acquires it and falls into the valleys of misguidance and perishes. For this reason it is said: Stupidity is closer to salvation than a defective intellect.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument for the obligation (wujūb) of acquiring knowledge of God (maʿrifat al-Ṣāniʿ). The author begins by stating that everything that repels suspected or even doubtful harm is rationally obligatory. He likens this to the case of a traveler who is warned of danger—even if the warning is uncertain, he must avoid it. He then applies this to the disagreement among sects, which leads to worldly harms. However, an objection is raised: the argument assumes that people are aware of this disagreement and its consequences, which is not always true. Moreover, even if they are aware, information about the Maker and the afterlife only reaches some, and even then there is no preponderance for truth because one does not know the truth of the message. Even if we grant the fear of harm, acquiring knowledge does not necessarily remove that fear because error remains possible. The objector then says: But knowledge makes one better, and attaining what is better is obligatory. The author responds: Yes, but only if knowledge is attained correctly—without certainty, it may lead to misguidance. Hence, defective intelligence (fiṭāna batrāʾ) is worse than simple-mindedness (balāha). The passage thus defends the rational obligation of seeking knowledge while acknowledging the risks of error.

Key Terms
معرفة الصانع
ضرر مظنون
واجب عقلاً
تحصيل المعرفة
فطانة بتراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any knowledge of God is automatically beneficial.
Correction: Sanusi shows that without certainty, acquired knowledge can lead to misguidance and harm; only proper knowledge is beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the author reject the claim that acquiring knowledge always improves one's state?
A) Because knowledge is never certain.
B) Because one may acquire knowledge incorrectly and fall into misguidance.
C) Because the objector is a heretic.
D) Because the obligation to acquire knowledge is only for scholars.

Key Takeaway
Seeking knowledge of God is rationally obligatory to avoid harm, but it must be pursued correctly to avoid misguidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard that avoiding harm obliges you to know God. In what specific area of your life have you been avoiding the hard work of seeking certain knowledge, thinking ignorance is safer? How will you change that this week?
?
Sanusi warns that a defective intellect can lead to perdition. Where in your pursuit of Islamic knowledge have you been proud of your own reasoning without grounding it in proper sources and methodology? Will you submit to sound learning, or continue risking misguidance?
?
The passage says 'stupidity is closer to salvation than a defective intellect.' Does this challenge your intellectual pride? Are you willing to admit where you lack certainty and seek help, or will you persist in your own flawed understanding?
